  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_a3e22a20-5814-4361-8cee-f2239238286a'
      });
    }

      $('rating_details_tip').observe('prototip:hidden', function () {
        $$('span.elementTwo').each(function (e) {
          if (e.getStyle('display') !== 'none') {
            var lessLink = e.next();
            swapContent(lessLink);
          }
        });
      });

<div id="review_116518161" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/116518161" />
    <a title="Suzanne" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3695758-suzanne"><img alt="Suzanne" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1369500256p2/3695758.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/116518161?book_show_action=true">Aug 12, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Suzanne" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Suzanne" href="/user/show/3695758-suzanne">Suzanne</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780316132763" href="/book/show/912648.Red_Earth_and_Pouring_Rain">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3695758-suzanne?shelf=metafictional-madness-and-pomo-fun">metafictional-madness-and-pomo-fun</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer116518161" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17913826091033922481">The main things this book had going for it:<br/>•	Stories within stories within stories. You might get lost like I did, but I found I didn’t really care about what level I was in as long as the stories kept unrolling and enrapturing me;<br/>•	Beautiful, vivid and lyrical language: descriptive, character-illuminating, sometimes philosophical;<br/>•	A typewriting monkey! I mean, a TYPEWRITING MONKEY! <br/><br/>So the stories -- lots of stories -– form a Scheherazade-style framework with the monkey telling tales to both vi</span>
  <span id="freeText17913826091033922481" style="display:none">The main things this book had going for it:<br />•	Stories within stories within stories. You might get lost like I did, but I found I didn’t really care about what level I was in as long as the stories kept unrolling and enrapturing me;<br />•	Beautiful, vivid and lyrical language: descriptive, character-illuminating, sometimes philosophical;<br />•	A typewriting monkey! I mean, a TYPEWRITING MONKEY! <br /><br />So the stories -- lots of stories -– form a Scheherazade-style framework with the monkey telling tales to both visiting gods and townspeople to save his own life: tales of romance, adventure, war, family, love, birth, death, and growing up, the magical and the mundane, from 18th century India to 1980s California. The framework around the stories, each of which has its own conflict and arc, has a conflict of its own, the challenge every storyteller has: to keep his audience intrigued. Or else. <br /><br /><b>Today the television cameras came, and also the death threats. We have been warned by several organizations that the storytelling must stop. The groups on the very far right – of several religions – object to the ‘careless use of religious symbology, and the ceaseless insults to the sensitivities of the devout.’ The far-left parties object to the sensationalization and falsification of history, and the pernicious Western influences on our young.’ Everyone objects to the sex, except the audience. <br /><br />We have become a national issue. Questions have been raised in parliament. Sir Patanjali Abhishek Vardarajan, the grand old man of Indian science, has offered a reward of fifty thousand rupees to ‘anyone who can demonstrate the existence of a typing monkey under laboratory conditions.’ . . . <br /><br />‘We will not be bullied,’ Saira said. “Type on.’</b> <br /><br />I was almost a hundred pages into this before I learned that Chandra had studied writing with John Barth at Johns Hopkins. Well, no wonder I was loving it. This has the same sort of sprawling scope and playful tone as many of Barth’s longer works, blending the mythical, historical and the everyday in a similar fashion. It’s certainly not on a level in terms of prose or structure, of course, but it’s still awfully good. And once I knew the connection, I could plainly see the master’s fingerprints, the DNA is there. <br /><br />I’m not going to lie to you. I didn’t enjoy every page of this. Sometimes the epic battle scenes were too detailed and the play-by-play of a cricket match left me restless and skimming. But then, I’m such a girl, I don’t enjoy war and sports that much. However, most of it was delightful. <br /><br />So there are stories within stories, which are also stories <b>about</b> stories. There’s a reverence for storytelling that permeates the whole, with the frequent interjection: <br /><br />	“Listen . . . . “<br /><br />And we do. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="17913826091033922481" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_9187815" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/9187815" />
    <a title="Don Dada" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/623960-don-dada"><img alt="Don Dada" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1195332919p2/623960.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/9187815?book_show_action=true">Nov 16, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Don Dada" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Don Dada" href="/user/show/623960-don-dada">Don Dada</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">everyone</span></div>

        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer9187815" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7965162138631909279">A young man returns to India after going to college in Los Angeles. While tangled in a web of identity issues, the young man shoots a monkey that had stolen his levis ( symbolism anyone?). This is a big no-no in their neck of the woods and the young man's family rushes to save the monkey.<br/><br/>While nursing the monkey back to health, it becomes clear that the shooting had flipped a switch in the monkey that allows him to remember his past life as a poet. The monkey proceeds to climb up to the typewrit</span>
  <span id="freeText7965162138631909279" style="display:none">A young man returns to India after going to college in Los Angeles. While tangled in a web of identity issues, the young man shoots a monkey that had stolen his levis ( symbolism anyone?). This is a big no-no in their neck of the woods and the young man's family rushes to save the monkey.<br /><br />While nursing the monkey back to health, it becomes clear that the shooting had flipped a switch in the monkey that allows him to remember his past life as a poet. The monkey proceeds to climb up to the typewriter and begin telling his story. Yama, the god of death, comes down and says, "Stop what you're doing, cuz I'm about to ruin the image and the style that you're used to" and tries to put an end to the monkey's ... er ...monkeying around. While Yama is dragging him from this mortal coil, the monkey prays to Hanuman (god of monkeys and poets) to save him. Hanuman kicks in the door wavin a .44 (not really, but i'm trying to keep the hip-hop/Hindu gods thing going) and talks Yama into a bet. They will assemble a crowd and the monkey will climb up to the typewriter and begin telling his story. If the crowd becomes bored at any point during his story, Yama can take the monkey with him. But if the monkey tells a good story, he gets to kick it. Deal. <br /><br />The monkey starts telling his story, but because he is still recovering from being shot, he needs the young man to pick up the slack - afterall, he's the one who got the monkey in this mess. So the young man sits down and begins telling his story about life in America. This is where the book actually begins.<br /><br /> If you can believe it, I have only told you the first 20 pages. The rest of the book is occupied by the two stories revolving around one another in a fairly astounding fashion. This is an incredible book. I cannot recommend it enough<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="7965162138631909279" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_106756433" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/106756433" />
    <a title="Caroline" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3858088-caroline"><img alt="Caroline" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1277161952p2/3858088.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/106756433?book_show_action=true">Jun 12, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Caroline" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Caroline" href="/user/show/3858088-caroline">Caroline</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780140246124" href="/book/show/511873.Red_Earth_and_Pouring_Rain">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer106756433" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5700516332207085178">Abhay comes home to India after studying in America, and he shoots a monkey that's been bothering the family for years. Wounded, they take the monkey in and nurse it, hopefully back to health. The monkey starts having flashbacks and realizes that it's a reincarnation of his former human self. the God of Death, Yama appears to the monkey, aka Sanjay in his former life, and wants to take him but Hanuman, the God of monkeys appears when he's appealed to by Sanjay. They strike a bargain and if Sanja</span>
  <span id="freeText5700516332207085178" style="display:none">Abhay comes home to India after studying in America, and he shoots a monkey that's been bothering the family for years. Wounded, they take the monkey in and nurse it, hopefully back to health. The monkey starts having flashbacks and realizes that it's a reincarnation of his former human self. the God of Death, Yama appears to the monkey, aka Sanjay in his former life, and wants to take him but Hanuman, the God of monkeys appears when he's appealed to by Sanjay. They strike a bargain and if Sanjay can keep an audience enthralled with stories for 2 hours a day, he will be allowed to live, perhaps in another life form, but at least out of the final clutches of Yama.<br /><br />Abhay and his family, including a precocious little girl, named Saira, are captivated by this monkey who can't speak (and we learn later why not) but who can type. Saira manages to gather up an audience of school children the next day when the story-telling sessions begin and what occurs next is a little like an Indian version of 1001 Arabian Nights.<br /><br />We are treated to the unfolding drama and saga of Sanjay's life, from the time before his mystical conception to his current condition. The stories are interspersed of course with breaks in time because there's only so much a monkey can type, and also the stories were supposed to only last for 2 hour sessions at a time. The stories include that of his equally mystically conceived brothers, Chotta and the famous warrior Sikander, and their journey from reckless boyhood, through harsh family trials, accidents, quests and wars between the Indians and their English masters. <br /><br />The stories are so well told that we are immersed in each moment, and forget that it's being told by a typing monkey.<br /><br />During the intervals between the passages of time in the stories, we are brought back to the present, and find that the elephant God, Ganesh, has joined the other 2 celestial beings, and there is light banter amongst them all. <br /><br />The only odd notes in this book was that the author felt the need to have Abhay contribute some of his own stories, of a portion of his life in America to the mix, ostensibly because Sanjay's monkey paws were cramping from prolonged typing. I thought his trite stories of college partying, some drug use, and road trips rather jarring to the overall lyrical tone of the book. Thankfully, there weren't too many of Abhay's stories to be too distracting.<br /><br />It is truly Sanjay's stories of his epic life journey that make this book a compelling read. </span>
  <a data-text-id="5700516332207085178" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3213631792" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3213631792" />
    <a title="Inderjit Sanghera" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/900800-inderjit-sanghera"><img alt="Inderjit Sanghera" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1564826235p2/900800.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3213631792?book_show_action=true">Mar 01, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Inderjit Sanghera" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Inderjit Sanghera" href="/user/show/900800-inderjit-sanghera">Inderjit Sanghera</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3213631792" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15312610005014213852">At some point in the novel the various narratives which form part of 'Red Earth and Pouring Rain' coalescence into a single story which has multiples essences but which centres on the theme of dislocation; the dislocation of Sanjay and Sikander as they are sundered from their families and martial cultures due to the expansion of colonial England, the dislocation of Abhay as he circumnavigates his way through late 20th century America and the vagaries of an India which seems increasingly surreal </span>
  <span id="freeText15312610005014213852" style="display:none">At some point in the novel the various narratives which form part of 'Red Earth and Pouring Rain' coalescence into a single story which has multiples essences but which centres on the theme of dislocation; the dislocation of Sanjay and Sikander as they are sundered from their families and martial cultures due to the expansion of colonial England, the dislocation of Abhay as he circumnavigates his way through late 20th century America and the vagaries of an India which seems increasingly surreal to him and the dislocation of a monkey, who is the reincarnation of a sagacious revolutionary, whose fight against the British took him from the printing press, to fratricide to a battle with the Jack the Ripper in the guise of an immortal and malevolent doctor. <br /><br />Throughout Chandra's prose is imbued with a kind of baleful beauty, as the novel perpetually teeters on the brink of violence under the eyes of its simian Scheherazade as he explores the conquest of the British from its early days, to the mutiny of 1847 to its height in late Victorian England as England grew fat on the riches it stole from its colonies. Amidst all this there exist beautiful princesses and legendary warriors,  familial betrayals and all of the other things which you would form part of great myths, yet Chandra subtly upends many of these myths; the great Rajput warrior Sikander ends up serving the very people he vowed to fight and the bookish intellectual Sanjay ends up sowing the seeds of revolution; the princesses, whilst being beautiful, are far from helpless and end up bewitching the mighty warriors who sought to ensure them and who end up being consumed with a sense of emptiness and ennui.<br /><br />It is difficult to sum up a novel as varies as Chandra's, a novel which has multiple different narrations, some of which are never picked up again and some of which lead nowhere, but there is something unmistakably brilliant about the novel as a whole, as Chandra explores over 200 years of Indian history and the sense of wonder and awe it creates in others.  </span>
  <a data-text-id="15312610005014213852" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_49030526" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/49030526" />
    <a title="Jeffrey Mervosh" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/761816-jeffrey-mervosh"><img alt="Jeffrey Mervosh" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1572619694p2/761816.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/49030526?book_show_action=true">Mar 12, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jeffrey Mervosh" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jeffrey Mervosh" href="/user/show/761816-jeffrey-mervosh">Jeffrey Mervosh</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/761816-jeffrey-mervosh?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/761816-jeffrey-mervosh?shelf=post-colonial-literature">post-colonial-literature</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer49030526" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8011808348132199846">This book is an endeavor. Written largely as a story-within-a-story, Red Earth and Pouring Rain relates the tales of two story-tellers - one, an Indian poet reincarnated in the form of a red monkey (whose human consciousness emerges after an accident), and the other, a newly-returned student sent to the United States for university. The two stories leapfrog back and forth, with each being told a chapter at a time. They tell the coming of age of two vastly different characters in vastly different</span>
  <span id="freeText8011808348132199846" style="display:none">This book is an endeavor. Written largely as a story-within-a-story, Red Earth and Pouring Rain relates the tales of two story-tellers - one, an Indian poet reincarnated in the form of a red monkey (whose human consciousness emerges after an accident), and the other, a newly-returned student sent to the United States for university. The two stories leapfrog back and forth, with each being told a chapter at a time. They tell the coming of age of two vastly different characters in vastly different periods of Indian history - both form complicated relationships with their own identity and with the people of Western backgrounds that surround them.<br /><br />Neither story has the weight to stand on its own - both feel rambling, and while there are definite high points, a great deal of momentum is lost by both the format and the tendency of the author to get lost in details and asides. As a result of three (or four) simultaneous storylines and ensembles, the cast of characters can get a bit confusing. Despite the great attention to detail and the ability of the author to capture emotion well, the protagonists are still largely shrouded in mystery. Even Sanjay, the central figure in the epic storyline with most depth, does not seem fully formed. His erratic behavior and vague value system can leave the reader befuddled as to his actions.<br /><br />Altogether, this book was interesting, and worth the read. I can't shake the feeling that the project was perhaps too ambitious, but there are threads of good stories here. The author is upfront that the format is that of mere story-telling; the narrative is borrowed from oral history and recollections of individual characters; it is not a true novel in that sense. There is a touch of the mythological that hints at weighty issues of meaning and justice, but these are largely circumnavigated by the angsty rebellion of Sanjay against the concept of fate. In the end, Vikram Chandra's work disappoints not because it is bad - but because it stops short of being something special. Like his characters, this work feels incomplete.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8011808348132199846" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer6526706999679051852"><i>Red Earth and Pouring Rain</i> is a whirlwind of a book, and a heavy whirlwind at that. The book itself is weighty, and its contents are jammed full of an overwhelming amount of characters.<br/><br/>I had trouble remembering who did what, what the effect was, and how things influenced each other later. So to me, it seemed in parts like one random thing happening after another. <br/><br/>I really enjoyed the first third or so of the book, and then I think things all started to jumble together. I think I finally lost tra</span>
  <span id="freeText6526706999679051852" style="display:none"><i>Red Earth and Pouring Rain</i> is a whirlwind of a book, and a heavy whirlwind at that. The book itself is weighty, and its contents are jammed full of an overwhelming amount of characters.<br /><br />I had trouble remembering who did what, what the effect was, and how things influenced each other later. So to me, it seemed in parts like one random thing happening after another. <br /><br />I really enjoyed the first third or so of the book, and then I think things all started to jumble together. I think I finally lost track of what was going on about halfway through. But I kept reading, hoping that everything would work itself out in the end. For me, it didn't. </span>

<div id="review_95163251" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/95163251" />
    <a title="Neha" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2327087-neha"><img alt="Neha" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1593193903p2/2327087.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/95163251?book_show_action=true">Mar 21, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Neha" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Neha" href="/user/show/2327087-neha">Neha</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2327087-neha?shelf=read-2010">read-2010</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2327087-neha?shelf=kwench">kwench</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2327087-neha?shelf=g-asia-india">g-asia-india</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer95163251&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer95163251" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6427714993513268400">Having read Vikram Chandra before it seemed to be a safe bet to pick up again. So I chose 'Red Earth & Pouring Rain' - the title seemed enticing and the brief seemed interesting with a monkey as the story teller and a lot of mythical characters thrown in to create the mysticism. <br/><br/>In 'Sacred Games' one realizes that Vikram believes in storytelling and has seriously attempted to do that, succeeding in some & failing in others , but in this book Vikram is all the time speaking about how storytelling</span>
  <span id="freeText6427714993513268400" style="display:none">Having read Vikram Chandra before it seemed to be a safe bet to pick up again. So I chose 'Red Earth &amp; Pouring Rain' - the title seemed enticing and the brief seemed interesting with a monkey as the story teller and a lot of mythical characters thrown in to create the mysticism. <br /><br />In 'Sacred Games' one realizes that Vikram believes in storytelling and has seriously attempted to do that, succeeding in some &amp; failing in others , but in this book Vikram is all the time speaking about how storytelling is so important and the single most goal of his book, but ends up telling about &amp; everything which adds up in nothing. There were too many stories just for the sake of count like say one narrator talks about this guy who becomes narrator for another guy in another story and so on and so forth. You don’t even realize when a story starts or ends infact there is no flow or depth in any story. The author has created sections in the book with certain themes like The Book of revenge or The book of mistakes, etc, but nowhere that theme is visible in the stories The stories break abruptly and get into other unrelated stories. The author has not even done enough character sketch or research as the characters keep jumping from one story to another, confusing as to who is who, and how did this guy land here whereas he was part of some other story. As per authors most of the characters are heroic with a mystic aura around them winning wards &amp; born to fulfill destinies, but the presentation and the flow of stories depict the characters as shallow &amp; lifeless. <br /><br />The book was a mixture of too many themes. At many places the author was trying too hard to create a Rushdie, with traces of Midnight's children or children born with super powers or labyrinth of stories, well here Vikram needs to be told that even Rushdie has not been able to create another Midnight's children so Vikram should not even try. At other places it seemed like a writing by one of the NRI authors like Jhumpa or Chitra or 100s of others who try to create the dilemmas of being an Indian or American and how they are neither. Sometimes Vikram also tried to create a mythical world of Indian Gods, usage of priests &amp; sages &amp; age old writings, but all these have been wasted entirely and these Gods seemed more like comedians than possessing any mysticism or powers. <br /><br />I would strictly advice not to read this &amp; waste your time. I had read most of it hoping for something to happen but it just didn’t. The climax is the biggest weakness of Vikram, he starts off well but he kills his stories with the kind of endings he writes, which are so drab &amp; dull. Vikram my advise to you would be – to really try your hands at short stories before writing something like an epic of short stories and then connecting them randomly. <br /><br /><br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="6427714993513268400" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">

        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/143313679?book_show_action=true">Jan 24, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Angela" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Angela" href="/user/show/4821718-angela">Angela</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer143313679" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1333760880672809776">I kind of feel like Vikram Chandra said to himself 'what do I want to read? what do I want to see happen in a story?' then gathered every idea he had ever had and smooshed it into a book. He covers every single genre in one way or another and at times it is so compacted that I couldnt remember who people were and why they were there so had to browse back through the first third of the book. At times it was very wordy, almost unnecessarily so, and I didnt think it was actually needed. Abhay's sto</span>
  <span id="freeText1333760880672809776" style="display:none">I kind of feel like Vikram Chandra said to himself 'what do I want to read? what do I want to see happen in a story?' then gathered every idea he had ever had and smooshed it into a book. He covers every single genre in one way or another and at times it is so compacted that I couldnt remember who people were and why they were there so had to browse back through the first third of the book. At times it was very wordy, almost unnecessarily so, and I didnt think it was actually needed. Abhay's story was so sharply contrasted with the rest of the book that Chandra could have left it out and still achieved the same outcome. I didnt see the point of Abhay's immature antics with his rich but miserable friends. It was a whole other book slid between a story of battle, Indian Gods, love and struggle. A book that was strangely put together, I probably would't read it again.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer7300900554275701015">I really enjoyed this. I don’t think I’ve ever read anything like it. A story within a story, within a story—sounds chaotic, yet it somehow captivated me and kept my attention. The flow of the novel worked. Some stories read like fables, others like scenes from a play. I usually read more than one book at a time but I didn’t find myself reaching for anything else while I was reading this. I would pick it up again. </span>

<div id="review_2399571358" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2399571358" />
    <a title="Praveen Palakkazhi" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/15444712-praveen-palakkazhi"><img alt="Praveen Palakkazhi" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1553096087p2/15444712.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2399571358?book_show_action=true">May 22, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Praveen Palakkazhi" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Praveen Palakkazhi" href="/user/show/15444712-praveen-palakkazhi">Praveen Palakkazhi</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780571203086" href="/book/show/165601.Red_Earth_and_Pouring_Rain">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/15444712-praveen-palakkazhi?shelf=read-reviewed">read-reviewed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/15444712-praveen-palakkazhi?shelf=indian-authors">indian-authors</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/15444712-praveen-palakkazhi?shelf=4-star">4-star</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2399571358" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17541223225459755097">Listen.-<br/><br/>I used to think that Sacred Games, which I had read almost a decade ago, was Vikram Chandra’s magnum opus. But, while that tale was a gripping thrill ride with wonderfully etched characters, at the end of the day it was a relatively easy to pin down cops and robbers saga in the gritty underbelly of Mumbai. <br/><br/>But this. This, his first novel published in the mid-nineties, has to be his epic. Starting with a wonderful nostalgia inducing title (for a non-resident Malayalee, the phrase evokes a</span>
  <span id="freeText17541223225459755097" style="display:none">Listen.-<br /><br />I used to think that Sacred Games, which I had read almost a decade ago, was Vikram Chandra’s magnum opus. But, while that tale was a gripping thrill ride with wonderfully etched characters, at the end of the day it was a relatively easy to pin down cops and robbers saga in the gritty underbelly of Mumbai. <br /><br />But this. This, his first novel published in the mid-nineties, has to be his epic. Starting with a wonderful nostalgia inducing title (for a non-resident Malayalee, the phrase evokes a certain place and a certain time with longing) that is taken from an ancient Tamil poem, this book is an ode to storytelling in its finest form. It gets convoluted, it gets messy and it gets utterly complex at times with the profusion of characters and myth intermingling to form a heady concoction of daze in the reader’s head. But it enthralls and entertains with its mix of history and magic realism, a proud nod to Rushdie and Marquez while wholly creating its own unique space with its drawing from Hindu traditions and tales to weave its story.<br /><br />The storytelling also takes its basic form from Scheherazade, she of the thousand and one nights and all. We have Abhay, a US educated youth returned from his American sojourn to his family home. He decides to put an end once and for all to the menace of a monkey who has been tormenting his family by stealing their food and clothes from the terrace for a while. His parents accept the monkey as a regular visitor, but Abhay decides to gun it down. While recovering, the monkey gains its senses and knowledge of its past life as Parasher, or Sanjay, a poet in nineteenth century India. Yama, the God of death, lies in wait for him, but with the help of Hanuman and Ganesha (who make appearances in the household too), they strike a bargain. If Sanjay can keep his audience entertained with a story for a certain period every day, Yama may just decide to relent. After the contract has been signed and the audience summoned, the monkey starts. Oh, and since he can’t really speak yet, he types it all out on the typewriter.<br /><br />The tale he tells delves into multiple layers and goes back into a history of colonialism and old world values, but centers mainly on Sanjay and his friend Sikander, named after the all-conquering (until he mysteriously retreated from India) Alexander of Macedonia. Their genesis itself is an act of wonder, a result of mysteriously assembled ladoos and a yearning erstwhile Rajput princess, now the wife/concubine of the conquering British officer. Prior to this we are introduced to an Irish sailor who adopts India as his homeland and fights wars which ultimately lead to his connection with the boys. There is also a Frenchman who becomes a leader of armies in India. There are characters and more characters. These sections of the story are interspersed with a contemporary account of Abhay’s travels and college life in the United States. There are stories which come alive within the main stories, like the sudden diversion into the life story of a prostitute Abhay and his friends come across, all delivered in a monologue. Towards the end it takes on further elements of the supernatural and also delves into thriller territory with a reference to the Jack the Ripper murders which gripped London at one time. <br /><br />It’s not a perfect book for sure. Some of the threads get too muddled on reading and it gets tough to remember all the myriad characters and incidents. Abhay’s narrative, while a nice detour from the heavy handedness of the historical tale, does not convey its message effectively enough, if there was one. It feels more like some random musings thrown together into the story. And it’s a long and exhausting read, only to be attempted if your attention span is capable of the heft this book needs and deserves.<br /><br />But, eventually, it’s a story about storytellers and as a character says, once the story has left your pen, it ceases to be yours and becomes the reader’s. And so it does, for us to listen and draw our own meanings from the inexhaustible well of wonder that words can provide. <br /><br />So. Listen.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17541223225459755097" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2397074108" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2397074108" />
    <a title="Sabeena" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7508838-sabeena"><img alt="Sabeena" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1526572520p2/7508838.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2397074108?book_show_action=true">Sep 14, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sabeena" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sabeena" href="/user/show/7508838-sabeena">Sabeena</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer2397074108&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer2397074108" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4987295926941097868">I won't repeat the synopsis here; everyone seems to have done that. I read this book to 300 pages and started to get a bit lost. So i went back 200 pages and reread ..*slowly* and carefully. You really need to invest your attention in the book otherwise its easy to get lost. There are two main narratives- Sanjay's (monkey) and Abhay's (recently returned from LA). I preferred the former and to be completely honest i skim-read through Abhay's stories a fair bit. <br/>What helped me to work through the </span>
  <span id="freeText4987295926941097868" style="display:none">I won't repeat the synopsis here; everyone seems to have done that. I read this book to 300 pages and started to get a bit lost. So i went back 200 pages and reread ..*slowly* and carefully. You really need to invest your attention in the book otherwise its easy to get lost. There are two main narratives- Sanjay's (monkey) and Abhay's (recently returned from LA). I preferred the former and to be completely honest i skim-read through Abhay's stories a fair bit. <br />What helped me to work through the book was making notes! There is so much going on and many characters and their stories to take stock of that i needed to write major events down so i could reference them if i got lost at any point as the book progressed. It seemed to work well. Although i was disappointed not to find a study guide or 'cliff notes' type reading guide to check against if i needed a bit more clarification. The tales are layered- sometimes storytelling within storytelling- but they are raw, powerful and epic. <br />Here are some of my notes (please feel free to correct me where i may have misinterpreted). I had wanted elaboration on a couple of things but havent been able to find the answers to those yet. <br />There are 5 'books'<br /><br /><br />1. The Book of War and Ancestors<br />2. The Book of Learning and Desolation <br />3. The Book of Blood and Journeys<br />4. The Book of Revenge and Madness <br />5. The Book of the Return <br /><br />Sanjay reveals story of Sandeep<br />↪Sandeep tells story to Shankar and the Sadhus.<br />He tells of a Sadhu woman he comes across in the forest. <br />Sadhu woman tells story of Benoit La Borgne. 'My Life has been a dream'. He was always 'staring into the distance, giving him an unintentional air of superiority'. <br />1768 he has an argument with a sardinian in Inn. <br />He has a 'Story of Alexander' book. <br />"Listen" he says to the 'circle of men' <br />In France, Mercenary ranks of Irish Brigade.<br />'He moved to Greece where Admiral Orloff was commanding a Russian force against Turks, in a war that has already passed out of memory and myth into the deathly still of libraries'.<br />1 month later he is sold as a slave to a Turkish noble.<br />Then released. He goes to Constantinople, Aleppo, Cairo, Madras, Calcutta, Lucknow then to Delhi. <br /><br />George Thomas story (Jahaj Jung)<br />Reinhardt the Sombre. <br />Zeb-ul-Nissa aka Begum Sumroo. Begum marries Reinhardt. Thomas leaves. Uday Singh. <br />Alexander the Macedon, The Great or Sikander the madman is being discussed by Ram Mohan and his brother in law, Arun. <br />Janvi (Hercules' wife) wants to hear about Alexander. She is pregnant. Shanti Devi (Ram Mohan's sister) arrives and seats Janvi down. <br />She has Hercules' daughters and sends Uday to Thomas to seek help (secretly) in conceiving boys. Thomas sends the magic ladoos. Janvi and Shanti devi consume the magic Laddoos. Janvi gives birth to:<br />1. Son (name unknown) the illuminous baby<br />(Later described as Tall, thin.and gone to sea)<br />2. Sikander (Rajput warrior)<br />3. Robert (chottu or chotta sikander)<br />Shanti devi and Arun's son: Sanjay born on the same night as Sikander. <br />"We'll call him Sanjay, after the one who closed his eyes and yet saw everything". <br /><br />Sanjay's accident falling on to trident. P.218. Hercules visits him speaking in English. Sanjay doesn't understand but has a thing for language and patterns. <br />'He wants us to give him the Vedas'<br />All refuse. You need to be 'born twice'<br />Sanjay's head shaved and he narrates the Vedas to Ram Mohan for transcribing. <br /><br />Hercules wants to send his daughters to Calcutta. Janvi refuses.<br />She takes her own life in protest. <br />Sanjay bands his eye with cloth.<br /><br />Ram Mohan relays story summary on p.292. Herculea was Janvi's (Jenny's) captor. She was forced to marry him. She loved Thomas. <br />After her death, Hercules arrests Ram Mohan and takes him away.<br /><br />1.5yrs later...<br />Sikander and Sanjay start working at the Markline Printing Press in Calcutta. <br />Calcutta- 'Black machinery and noise'<br />Sikander as Manager<br />Sanjay assists with printing, setting the type.<br />P.311 ink ball rollers, press pullers.<br />Twins<br />Sanjay wants to learn to read english. <br />Markline asks him what he wants to do, Sanjay says 'be a writer' but he actually wanted to say 'a poet'<br />Markline betrays Sanjay by writing something about Janvi's suicide that was not true. In that, she was regressive and didnt want her husband to send off his daughters to become educated, civilised. <br />Civilization dicay (sic)<br />Sanjay takes revenge by eating the type and altering the book.<br /><br />Sanjay and Sikander leave the Printers. Head to Lucknow. "I wish to be a writer. I wish to have women" - sanjay.<br />In Lucknow they found a city mad with poetry. <br />They steal food and run. Sunil takes them in, shares food and stories.<br />Sanjay begins writing poetry.<br />They go to greet Uday Singh. And meet with Begum Sumroo. 'You are the witch of Sardhana'.<br /><br />4th book Abhay's story.<br /><br />Next, Begum Sumroo gets jobs for Sikander and Sanjay. Sanjay meets Gul Jahaan (courtesan). She is older. He falls for her. <br />Sanjay's instructors are two gentlemen. One is Hart Sahib.<br />Sanjay visits the library. P.437. He begins studying English under Hart Sahib. <br /><br />Back to Sandeep. He relays that:<br />Sanjay published his first collection of poetry and letters arrive from Sikander (who has returned to Calcutta for Rajput duties).<br />In his letters he tells Sanjay of his battles, of Thomas and finally of Thomas' death. <br />Sikander has married his 7th time.<br />Then he serves the English. <br />Sanjay has a child and takes him to his parents for them to take care of him. <br />Sarthy's diary.<br />Is Sanjay Thomas and Begus Sumroo's son????<br />Sanjay in the cave. Loses his tongue and fingers. Re-emerges 32yrs 3months and 2 days later and is found by Sunil.<br />Everyone has died. Sikander and Chottu are still alive.<br />Chotta tells story of 'what has become of us'.<br />Sanjay wants revenge from the English, and Sarthy. Sikander has loyalty to them.<br />They both fight.<br />Sikander is killed by Sanjay.<br />Sikander's men kill Sanjay but Yama says 'you're the one with unfinished business'. He is alive. <br />Off to London.<br />He looks saintly (white beard, doesnt speak). Near kabul a chieftain enslaves him for 13yrs. When chieftain dies, Sanjay goes on to Persia. Later he poses as a sardinian officer and gets visa stamped. P.547.<br />London.<br />He is looking for Sarthy who is extemely old. But Sarthy has attained his wish; he cannot be killed.<br />Last book. <br />Game of Cricket<br />Coyote and the wolf<br />Abhay tells his story and he has returned to india from LA (back to beginning/ full circle)<br />Monkey shooting<br />Bomb on maidan- only hurts Saira<br />Sanjay stops telling stories and lays on Yama's lap. He says to abhay 'help her' and then dies. Abhay weeps for the monkey. <br /><br />Abhay has to tell stories to keep Saira alive. <br />The end.<br /><br />One of my questions was: Was Sanjay George Thomas and Begum Sumroo's son? What about his other set of parents? Was there some magic involved here.<br /><br />There were a few more but i wont go into them here.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4987295926941097868" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_16358397" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/16358397" />
    <a title="Heather Knight" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/620410-heather-knight"><img alt="Heather Knight" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1201472787p2/620410.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/16358397?book_show_action=true">Feb 25, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Heather Knight" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Heather Knight" href="/user/show/620410-heather-knight">Heather Knight</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer16358397" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17535460675989922035">This book is epic in scope, a story within a story, within a story sort of construction. The basic premise is that Sanjay, re-incarnated as a typewriting monkey, needs to spend two hours every day entertaining an ever-growing crowd of listeners with the story of his life, lest he be taken by Yama, god of death. He is helped in this endeavor mostly by Abhay, home from college, Abhay's parents, and a young neighbor girl. <br/><br/>So, knowing that the main character is a monkey who can type, there's obvious</span>
  <span id="freeText17535460675989922035" style="display:none">This book is epic in scope, a story within a story, within a story sort of construction. The basic premise is that Sanjay, re-incarnated as a typewriting monkey, needs to spend two hours every day entertaining an ever-growing crowd of listeners with the story of his life, lest he be taken by Yama, god of death. He is helped in this endeavor mostly by Abhay, home from college, Abhay's parents, and a young neighbor girl. <br /><br />So, knowing that the main character is a monkey who can type, there's obviously a precedent for magical realism set at the very start. It follows throughout as women become impregnated by magical sweets, immortality waxes and wanes and white-hot infants burn upon birth. Still, when Sanjay's nemesis turns out to be Jack the Ripper, I felt more than a little twinge of disbelief. <br /><br />Sanjay's stories are of the epic, Indian style. Abhay's stories are like the things self-obsessed rich brats at my college used to write in creative writing class, and his parents stories are fairy tales. All woven together, they are like reading several books at once.<br /><br />When you consider, however, that the book has at its heart, the concepts of birth and rebirth, karma, dharma and interconnection, the choice of format makes a lot of sense. The stories actually dovetail nicely at the end, bringing us back to the beginning and mirroring the cyclical procession that is life for Hindu believers.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="17535460675989922035" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3288145457?book_show_action=true">Sep 04, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dree" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dree" href="/user/show/837466-dree">Dree</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/837466-dree?shelf=south-asia">south-asia</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/837466-dree?shelf=2020-reads">2020-reads</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3288145457" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8315752894715012939">This novel flip flops between early 19th century India, and 1990s India and California/Texas. It is about coming home and what is home, family and what is family, and identity. The cover is not lying, there is a typing monkey.<br/><br/>Many of Chandra's early 19th century characters were real people. I am not well-versed in Indian history (especially Indian military history), so I spent a fair amount of time reading about James Sikander Skinner, George Thomas (Jaharai Jung), Begum Sumroo, and more on Wiki</span>
  <span id="freeText8315752894715012939" style="display:none">This novel flip flops between early 19th century India, and 1990s India and California/Texas. It is about coming home and what is home, family and what is family, and identity. The cover is not lying, there is a typing monkey.<br /><br />Many of Chandra's early 19th century characters were real people. I am not well-versed in Indian history (especially Indian military history), so I spent a fair amount of time reading about James Sikander Skinner, George Thomas (Jaharai Jung), Begum Sumroo, and more on Wikipedia. I looked up foods and places and wars. I am sure that someone with good knowledge of Indian history and culture would get a lot more out of this book than I did. <br /><br />But I did enjoy this. Stories within stories, characters across time, gods, cricket in Houston, college in LA, driving long distances, characters in the 19th century and the 20th century wondering who they are and where they belong.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8315752894715012939" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer70573810" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9110758234270398153">These things are known about this novel: epic scope, stories-within-stories, modern US subjects, and the history of India. More deeply, it explores the British invasion/influence/damage and how so many stereotypes perpetuated by Europeans persist today. Even more deeply, the fictional characters brought to life by Chandra are fascinating and human in every way. The art of story telling is treated with the greatest respect. Some of the Hindu gods appear as characters themselves--some delightful, </span>
  <span id="freeText9110758234270398153" style="display:none">These things are known about this novel: epic scope, stories-within-stories, modern US subjects, and the history of India. More deeply, it explores the British invasion/influence/damage and how so many stereotypes perpetuated by Europeans persist today. Even more deeply, the fictional characters brought to life by Chandra are fascinating and human in every way. The art of story telling is treated with the greatest respect. Some of the Hindu gods appear as characters themselves--some delightful, some fearful. This is a very long book, but it met my criteria for Indian literature: I learned a tremendous amount about the history, culture, and people of India (of which there are, like in the novel itself, many layers and stories behind stories).</span>

<div id="review_17064264" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/17064264" />
    <a title="Dennis" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/962322-dennis"><img alt="Dennis" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1430584962p2/962322.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/17064264?book_show_action=true">Mar 05, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dennis" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dennis" href="/user/show/962322-dennis">Dennis</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">anyone with an imagination</span></div>

        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer17064264" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer67537595285043296">What an awesome read, one of those "magic realism" books that take you places where your imagination wouldn't even dream of taking you. This sort of novel, where what is real, what is surreal and what is legend, all intertwine to the point of being indistinguishable seems to be a specialty of Indian and South American literature and I admit, I suck it up. An Indian warrior reincarnated as a typewriting monkey, relating his life story while a team of gods and mortals look on... wow!</span>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

<div id="review_307491443" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/307491443" />
    <a title="Matt Gough" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8620410-matt-gough"><img alt="Matt Gough" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1333696354p2/8620410.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/307491443?book_show_action=true">Apr 05, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Matt Gough" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Matt Gough" href="/user/show/8620410-matt-gough">Matt Gough</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer307491443" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10736347801505279822">A long, sprawling book, full of magical realism and adventure. At first, I wasn’t sure why there was a story about India and a modern story about an Indian guy in college in the U.S., but in the end it all comes together. A monkey writes about his former life as an Indian growing up during English colonialism and how he battles to be free of English rule. A lot of it is heavily symbolic and difficult to figure out at times, but it made for an interesting story overall.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3427656996" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3427656996" />
    <a title="Robert" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5926188-robert"><img alt="Robert" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1565871726p2/5926188.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3427656996?book_show_action=true">Jul 27, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Robert" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Robert" href="/user/show/5926188-robert">Robert</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3427656996" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13731939778997510238"><br/>Usually, if a book is going to lose me, it is going to do it in the first hundred pages. But this book was strange in a few ways, one of them being that at first, it was excellent, and it lost me much later, for reasons that were frustrating. <br/>Off the bat, Chandra’s writing is intelligent, well- paced, engaging and full of thoughtful perspectives. The ‘story within a story’ is a fun plot device and I enjoyed the challenge of it. At first. But after a few hundred pages I noticed that I just wasn’t</span>
  <span id="freeText13731939778997510238" style="display:none"><br />Usually, if a book is going to lose me, it is going to do it in the first hundred pages. But this book was strange in a few ways, one of them being that at first, it was excellent, and it lost me much later, for reasons that were frustrating. <br />Off the bat, Chandra’s writing is intelligent, well- paced, engaging and full of thoughtful perspectives. The ‘story within a story’ is a fun plot device and I enjoyed the challenge of it. At first. But after a few hundred pages I noticed that I just wasn’t interested in picking it up. Why? I actually couldn’t figure out what it was. Was I just in a bad mood? (I was, but that wasn’t it). Was it a bad book? No, not really. Was the writing poor? Not at all. Was I disengaged with the plot? No, I love stuff about India in just about any way. <br />But after thinking about it for a while and talking it out – I think the problem I was finding with this book is, to simplify it, that Chandra just tries to do way, waaayy too much here. And though he is excellent and intelligent, he is just not a brilliant enough writer to pull it off. It smacks of trying to show off – he is not just having a story within a story, but – “I’ll show them!” - yet another story within that, and then… more sub-stories… within … them?? It ends up feeling choppy. Suddenly a character gets a letter from someone which is an entirely new short-story with new characters in it. And it’s never clear which ones are truly relevant. By page 500, one has the feeling that this is the longest book in the world, even though it isn’t that long. It just meanders so much, without ever fulfilling enough promise that you stop to care. <br />And the plot, that swaps back and forth from the present day India, to very recnet America, to India in the early 1800’s, does so in a totally lopsided way – and though he does tie things together in the end, the threads are thematic and not direct enough to be satisfying. I really loved the present day America sections – I felt they were sincere and beautiful, and I read those parts intently. However, some of the sections of describing the wars, for example, felt utterly superfluous. <br />And I guess that was the odd thing about this book – it was so choppy that I loved certain parts, while other huge swaths of the book I started skimming over. And I noticed that I wasn’t losing anything at all by selectively doing that. (or so it seems). <br />I was hoping to read Sacred Games, Chandra’s much more popular recent novel, but I think he broke my trust here. <br />All in all, certainly don’t recommend unless you really want to dig into pre-/ and colonializing India. Mostly the book is about the wars and india v. English drama of the time. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="13731939778997510238" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2245505279" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2245505279" />
    <a title="Milind Hegde" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/25502334-milind-hegde"><img alt="Milind Hegde" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1514922992p2/25502334.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2245505279?book_show_action=true">Mar 25, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Milind Hegde" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Milind Hegde" href="/user/show/25502334-milind-hegde">Milind Hegde</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/25502334-milind-hegde?shelf=indian-writing">indian-writing</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2245505279" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10628565453894186412">This was an interesting book. I found it quite difficult to follow - perhaps because I read it on and off over several months, but I've done the same with Vikram Chandra's other novel (Sacred Games, which I enjoyed much more) without a problem. <br/><br/>This book is written as a story within a story for several layers, as is common in Indian storytelling traditions - while this made it a little difficult to follow, for me this was not the main issue. The main story about Sanjay and his life, which runs t</span>
  <span id="freeText10628565453894186412" style="display:none">This was an interesting book. I found it quite difficult to follow - perhaps because I read it on and off over several months, but I've done the same with Vikram Chandra's other novel (Sacred Games, which I enjoyed much more) without a problem. <br /><br />This book is written as a story within a story for several layers, as is common in Indian storytelling traditions - while this made it a little difficult to follow, for me this was not the main issue. The main story about Sanjay and his life, which runs through the whole book, is what I found difficult. I didn't have an understanding of what the book is trying to do with the story; I couldn't understand Sanjay's motivations, which seemed to change substantially and gain new drivers in drastically different directions every so often. Perhaps it was this aspect which some reviewers apparently called "cinematic".<br /><br />I noticed a couple stories which are inversions of familiar stories from Hindu mythology and are nice winks from the author to the aware reader - I don't know how many I missed. The example that comes to mind is Sikander narrating his archery lesson where his master has him aim at a bird and then asks him what he sees; when Sikander replies that he sees only the bird, his master tells him that he will only not miss when he sees everything, the mountains and the sky and the ground and the bird as well. This is an exact inversion of the story from the Mahabharata of Dronacharya teaching the Panadavas and Kauravas archery. He asks each of them what they see, and all but Arjuna describe the bird and other things as well. Drona is pleased with only Arjuna, who claims to see nothing but the eye of the bird.<br /><br />The bits of the book I enjoyed the most were the parts that happened in present times: the descriptions of the situation in the maidan where the highest level of storytelling is happening, and all of Abhay's stories of his times in America. For me these are the parts which flowed the most easily, where it was easiest to empathise with the characters and find the humour and just-feels-right observations that make good writing good. This writing also feels the most down-to-earth; somehow in making Sanjay's thread somewhat grand and far-away, the writing gains some pretenses it could do without. Perhaps this is what makes Sacred Games so good.<br /><br />All in all: good, but read Sacred Games.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10628565453894186412" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2788342803" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2788342803" />
    <a title="Duane" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/9703288-duane"><img alt="Duane" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1588190365p2/9703288.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2788342803?book_show_action=true">May 05, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Duane" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Duane" href="/user/show/9703288-duane">Duane</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/12237980-red-earth-and-pouring-rain">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2788342803" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7049435473459885280"><a target='_blank' rel='noopener nofollow' href='https://barharukiya.wordpress.com/2019/05/01/red-earth-and-pouring-rain-vikram-chandra/'>https://barharukiya.wordpress.com/201...</a><br/><br/>Red Earth and Pouring Rain, on its surface, is the tale of a young Indian man, Aphay, who having returned to India from America, shoots a monkey who regularly visits his parental home for food, however, a spirit is awoken within the monkey as it is nursed back to health by Aphay’s parents, it is the spirit of Sanjay Parashar a poet who has to bargain with the Gods to extend his life, his payment? To tell a story and keep Lord Yama at bay.<br/><br/>His story is told </span>
  <span id="freeText7049435473459885280" style="display:none"><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://barharukiya.wordpress.com/2019/05/01/red-earth-and-pouring-rain-vikram-chandra/">https://barharukiya.wordpress.com/201...</a><br /><br />Red Earth and Pouring Rain, on its surface, is the tale of a young Indian man, Aphay, who having returned to India from America, shoots a monkey who regularly visits his parental home for food, however, a spirit is awoken within the monkey as it is nursed back to health by Aphay’s parents, it is the spirit of Sanjay Parashar a poet who has to bargain with the Gods to extend his life, his payment? To tell a story and keep Lord Yama at bay.<br /><br />His story is told throughout the book, which jumps back and forth over a couple of centuries covering everything from pre-English rule Hindustan, prior to Sanjay’s birth, the key figures at that time, both real and fictional, through his birth alongside his “brothers” Sikander and Chotto through to Aphay’s time studying in America, his falling in love but ultimately in him returning home and his fateful conflict with a white monkey who had stolen his jeans from the washing line.<br /><br />This is over simplifying the tale that is told within the pages of Chandra’s writings, even referring to it as a “tale” is to also over simplify things, as Red Earth and Pouring Rain is a whole bunch of tales with a wide variety of key characters and voices all told within an amalgamation of tales themselves. We are told of the Gods, of war, heroes and villains, love, death and everything in between and in the hands of a lesser author it could all have so easily have gone awry. That’s not to say that Red Earth and Pouring Rain isn’t a challenging read, it can be difficult to keep up with who is who and what has happened to each of the characters and at what point in history certain events take place. I also felt I may have had more of a grasp of things if I’d had more of an education in regards to India, its history and the religious upbringings of its people, as thing stand we’re not even taught about England’s involvement in the area (and it wasn’t until the recent episode of Doctor Who that I knew anything of the Partition of India for example) so reading this has been an eye opener really.<br /><br />I’ve struggled to take much more from the it really, I’m not sure if its trying to say anything in particular, and again, I can’t help but thinking that my very white working class upbringing has played its hand there, not that that excuses my ignorance, but I’m definitely glad that Red Earth and Pouring Rain came up as otherwise I wouldn’t have known is existence, let alone read it, as its most definetly outside of the kind of book I’d normally pick up and read.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7049435473459885280" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('8DZkbMcrYhxSXl2iWt7EKqPQXkywp+A4YK0CgJELb0N3nPKilKoQQ2gLuKQ0iydF1av3XrTv2YxhjI5NK8owkQ==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {

  }
